91,2026-07-19,"OnePlus is dead – actually for real this time. It’s got me looking back on my experiences with the brand’s smartphones, the absolute roller coaster that it was, and how OnePlus briefly sparked joy in a way that no one else way, resulting in two of my favorite Android phones of all time. And dang it, I’m a little sad now.
This issue of 9to5Google Weekender is a part of our newsletter that highlights the biggest Google stories with added commentary and more. Sign up here to get Weekender delivered to your inbox every weekend, and catch our weekday roundups too!
To quickly recap this week’s news, OnePlus is dead. At least in the US and Europe, that is. Oppo is pulling the plug and India is apparently next on the chopping block despite what OnePlus has said. Software updates will continue, but OxygenOS is going away and all OnePlus devices will move to ColorOS starting with Android 17.
The writing has been on the wall here for a long time. The “enthusiast brands will betray you” video came true. OnePlus long ago abandoned its roots and effectively turned into Oppo for the rest of the world. As a result, many of the things everyone liked about OnePlus in the early days were diluted to the point of being borderline unrecognizable – remember how OnePlus started with CyanogenMod (aka enhanced stock Android) before turning into Oppo’s clone? Those were the days, and they’re long gone.
There were some highlights, and they’ll vary from person to person. Some adored OnePlus’ hardware, others the software. 
For me, what I loved and will miss about OnePlus boils down to two releases in particular, both during the little hot streak that I’d hoped would reignite OnePlus as a brand – womp womp.
The OnePlus Open was, and frankly still is, one of the best foldables ever released. At the time it was on the absolute bleeding edge of hardware, offering a form factor that was thin enough without sacrificing battery capacity or even camera hardware – I’d argue the Open’s camera still outclasses Samsung’s latest foldables. It also brought some new ideas to life, such as “Open Canvas” which lives on in Oppo’s foldables, and is still the best way to multitask on a foldable screen. The whole idea there is to pretend there’s more screen than there actually is, and let the user quickly swap back and forth between apps. It’s brilliant, and while Android has implemented a version of this, but it’s not quite the same.
Close behind that is the OnePlus 13.
For the first time in roughly a decade, this is a device where OnePlus convinced me they could build an inherently good smartphone. Years of missteps on software, cameras, pricing, or any other number of factors always left OnePlus smartphones in this weird spot where I never preferred to use them over others. But the OnePlus 13 really changed my mind.
The OnePlus 13 was really just a winner across the board. Absolutely stellar battery life that, even now, no other Android flagship in the US can match. Excellent performance and thermal management, with the Oppo-fication of OxygenOS in a pretty good spot prior to everyone racing to copy Apple’s Liquid Glass. Shockingly great cameras that had an actual opinion and some life to them, all at a time where my usual Pixel was just draining the life out of every shot. And, to top it all off, hardware that both looked and felt incredible.
As I said in my review, this is the one that finally lived up to the whole “Never Settle” mantra.
There are other highlights over the years. The OnePlus 7T stands out to me as a good memory, as does the OnePlus 3. But these two in particular really hit the nail on the head. One last hurrah, you could say, as Oppo later ended up keeping its increasingly excellent foldables to itself, while the OnePlus 15 was a noticeable downgrade in everything but battery life. Really, the OnePlus 13 was the end of the OnePlus we wanted to love as everything quickly started going downhill quickly after.
And now it’s just over. No final sendoff, nothing to really “end” the brand’s legacy. Just a flagship that was widely dismissed as a misfire.
This is how OnePlus ends; “not with a bang, but a whimper.”
The writing has been on the wall for years. OnePlus was constantly stepping further and further out of the spotlight, and the US, the one thing that made OnePlus stand out amongst Oppo’s other brands, was less and less of a priority – no one needed a sloppy AI to confirm it, but as the signs became all the more clear, we were always headed for this outcome.
So, RIP OnePlus. 
As the US market for Android phones looks bleaker than ever, I’ll certainly miss OnePlus, especially following that hot streak between the Open and the 13. 
What do you think of the shutdown? Do you still use a OnePlus device?

92,2026-07-19,"
						By Ed Hardy • 7:00 am, July 19, 2026

Apple TV finally gets the audience it deserves. Or the streaming service is headed in that direction, anyway. In the contest to become the top streamer, Apple TV posted the strongest year-over-year growth of any platform last quarter.
Five shows helped push the streaming service’s audience numbers upward. Anyone looking for the best that Apple TV has to offer needs to be watching these.

Apple TV is Apple’s subscription streaming service, offering exclusive original TV shows, movies, documentaries and children’s programming. Since its 2019 launch, the service has built its reputation around high-quality, star-powered productions.
The video service started small. It made up a mere 3% of the U.S. streaming market in 2021, but grew to 7% by late 2023 as hits like Silo, Hijack and Shrinking attracted new viewers. However, growth then slowed through 2024 and early 2025, with Apple largely stuck at 7% to 8% market share.
But it rocketed up to 13% of the U.S. market over the past year, according to a Q2 2026 report from streaming guide JustWatch. That was enough for Apple TV to overtake both Hulu and HBO Max and become the fourth-largest subscription video-on-demand (SVOD) service in the United States.
The latest figures suggest Apple’s strategy of focusing on a smaller library of premium original programming has really started to pay off. Unlike its rivals, it didn’t buy a massive content library from outside sources. Instead, Apple spent years producing show after show that viewers love … and so do critics.
A 5-percentage-point gain in just a year in the cutthroat streaming market is extraordinary. But the success can be chalked up to some very popular shows.
Apple TV viewership a year ago would have still been riding high off the success of Severance season 2. This creepy workplace drama turned into a genuine streaming phenomenon following its long-awaited return in early 2025. The series dominated social media conversations, generated weekly speculation about its mysteries and consistently ranked among the most popular shows on Apple TV.
It became Apple’s biggest original series to date, even outperforming the previous flagship series, Ted Lasso.
For those who have somehow missed out, Severance is a psychological thriller set at a mysterious corporation where employees undergo brain surgery to separate their work memories from their personal lives. That creates two distinct versions of each person, setting the stage for a story filled with mystery, suspense and questions about identity and free will.
After its fall 2025 launch, Pluribus soon became a breakout hit thanks to its blend of high-concept science fiction, dark humor and compelling characters. Created by Breaking Bad and Better Call Saul mastermind Vince Gilligan, the series generated buzz as viewers embraced its unusual premise and unpredictable storytelling.
Critics praised Pluribus‘ originality and performances — it’s up for 18 Emmys! — while audiences helped turn it into one of Apple TV’s biggest successes of the past year, cementing its status as another prestige hit for the streaming service.
Pluribus follows the most miserable person on Earth, who’s tasked with saving the world from a contagion of hive-mind happiness.
Spring 2026 brought another Apple TV hit: Widow’s Bay draws viewers with its blend of supernatural mystery, horror and dark comedy. The series generated strong buzz with its eerie atmosphere, sharp writing and standout performances, while critics praised its fresh take on small-town secrets and the supernatural.
Its popularity with both audiences and awards voters helped make it one of Apple TV’s defining new series, culminating in a leading 19 Emmy nominations that underscored its broad appeal.
We can’t give more details on the show without spoilers … you just have to watch!
With two seasons under its belt as of this spring, Your Friends & Neighbors achieved long-term popularity thanks to its mix of dark humor, suspense and biting social commentary. Starring Jon Hamm (Mad Men) as a disgraced hedge fund manager who turns to stealing from his wealthy neighbors, the drama drew viewers with its sharp writing, morally complex characters and unexpected twists.
Strong word of mouth brought in audiences, and a recent Emmy nomination for Outstanding Drama Series certainly didn’t hurt.
Over three seasons, Shrinking became one of Apple TV’s most consistently popular series thanks to its winning blend of heartfelt drama and sharp comedy. Starring Jason Segel as a grieving therapist who begins telling his patients exactly what he thinks, alongside Harrison Ford in one of his first major television roles, the series earned widespread praise for its humor, emotional depth and memorable performances.
Great reviews and an expanding audience over multiple seasons make Shrinking one of Apple TV’s signature comedies. It finally broke into Nielsen’s U.S. streaming Top 10 after the premiere of season three, showing it has continued to grow rather than fade.
Some Apple TV series possess long-term appeal, still bringing in viewers even as audiences wait for new episodes.
Dystopian sci-fi drama Silo is Apple’s biggest ongoing audience hit, repeatedly appeared in Nielsen’s Top 10 streaming originals. It just returned to Apple TV on July 3 for season three after a wait of 18 months.
Breakout soccer comedy Ted Lasso hasn’t released a new episode since 2023, but remains one of Apple’s most-streamed series. Season four begins August 5!        
Ed Hardy has been writing full-time about tech for 26 years, and using it for much longer than that. His intro to Apple was a Macintosh SE/30 (which he still has), but now he uses a 13-inch iPad Pro as his primary computer.
That’s because he’s a “tablet first” type of guy. Rather than use a Macbook, he connects a keyboard case to the iPad. And instead of a desktop Mac, he connects his tablet to a 27-inch display and full-size keyboard. (So don’t try to tell him that everyone has to use a Mac to be productive.)
Before coming to Cult of Mac, Ed wrote for NotebookReview, TabletPCReview and Brighthand, as well as other sites.

93,2026-07-19,"This 1959 Mercedes-Benz 190Db is one of 711 produced between 1958 and 1961 with “kombiwagen” coachwork from Karosserie Binz. The car is said to have been acquired in the mid-1960s by a Southern California doctor who later moved it to his avocado farm, where it remained in storage from 1985 until being purchased by its previous owners in 2010. Its subsequent refurbishment, completed in September 2019, involved a repaint in the factory shade of Silver Gray Metallic, interior reupholstery in red leather, and installing a new-old-stock 1.9-liter inline-four crate engine, which is paired with a floor-shifted four-speed manual transmission. The brakes, suspension, exhaust system, wiring, and other areas were also serviced. The vehicle was acquired by the seller on BaT in February 2020, and subsequent work has included repairing its odometer, installing a rebuilt Becker Mexico radio, and updating its glow-plug system. This W121 Kombi is offered with owner’s manuals, records from current ownership, and a clean Michigan title in the seller’s name.

During the refurbishment completed in 2019, the body was disassembled and soda-blasted before being repainted in its factory Silver Gray Metallic (180), and a new gray canvas sunroof panel was installed. Additional work included repairing dings and replacing floor pans, rubber seals, and the rear quarter windows.

The 13″ steel wheels feature hubcaps with painted centers and are mounted with 175/70 tires. Lighting includes rebuilt European-market headlights as well as Hella fog lamps and fender-mounted turn signals, and the car also features dual side mirrors and Bosch fanfare horns. Noted flaws are touched-up paint chips, a scratch on the left rear door, and a crack in the corner of the left rear window.

The seats are reupholstered in red leather, while square-weave carpets line the floors, and the cabin has refinished wood trim as well as a gray flannel headliner. Sourced from Becker AutoSound of Saddle Brook, New Jersey, a replacement Becker Mexico two-piece, vacuum-tube radio and associated components were installed in 2022.

The five-digit odometer was repaired after the car was acquired by the seller, who added approximately 250 of the 3,400 kilometers (~2,100 miles) shown. Total mileage is unknown.

The 1.9-liter inline-four features an OM621.910 short block, which was reportedly acquired from a collector as a factory-prepared crate engine and then fitted with a rebuilt cylinder head from Metric Motors. A new wiring harness and exhaust system were also installed under previous ownership, and the fuel tank was cleaned and sealed, the radiator and heater core were refurbished, and fresh rubber hoses were fitted. Under current ownership, a glow-plug kit with parallel wiring and pencil-style plugs was fitted, and the battery was replaced in preparation for the sale.

Power is routed to the rear wheels through a floor-shifted four-speed manual transmission, which was equipped during the refurbishment with a rebuilt clutch and pressure plate, as well.

The VIN on the Michigan title is 1211021000047.

Here is the definition of the word “rumor” according to Merriam Webster dictionary:
Pronunciation: \ˈrü-mər\
Function: noun
Etymology: Middle English rumour, from Anglo-French, from Latin rumor clamor, gossip; akin to Old English rēon to lament, Sanskrit rauti he roars
Date: 14th century
1: talk or opinion widely disseminated with no discernible source
2: a statement or report current without known authority for its truth
3 archaic : talk or report of a notable person or event
